    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="9cc957b9338e4c4aaf6b6809ced2558a">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: 'bbee0b0c-2d8e-46e5-addd-126d3e734bfb'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

   <div class="abstract-group ">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d9736433" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) are expected to be widely used in low-speed vehicles and distributed energy storage devices due to their merits of abundance in natural resources, wide distribution, and low toxicity of raw materials. However, the performance of the state-of-the-art SIBs is restricted by the lack of ideal cathode materials. Iron-based polyanionic compounds have attracted much attention as potential SIBs cathode due to their low cost, excellent cycling stability, safety, and environmental friendliness, among which Na<sub>2</sub>Fe<sub>2</sub>(SO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> and Na<sub>4</sub>Fe<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>(P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub>) are the most commercializable representatives. Nevertheless, both academic and industrial communities hold a wait-and-see attitude. Herein, each of the aspects of crystal structure, synthesis methods, and electrochemical properties are first introduced. Then, the optimized schemes to improve electrochemistry, hygroscopicity, and conductivity for both are summarized. Finally, according to the current practical demand for iron-based polyanionic compounds, their application prospects and the corresponding bottleneck issues are comprehensively analyzed.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adfm202411280-sec-0010">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adfm202411280-sec-0010-title">1 Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>In the context of one global community of shared future, environmental issues such as the greenhouse effect, ozone layer depletion, and acid rain have received widespread attention. In comparison to fossil energy, clean energy sources such as solar and wind power have a minimal negative impact on the environment.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202411280-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202411280-bib-0001_R_d9736424e500" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Int. J. Sustain. Dev. World Ecol.">1</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, these natural energy sources are characterized by indirectness and discontinuity, which make them challenging to apply directly in real-world scenarios. A reasonable configuration of energy storage devices will be able to solve the above problems, which is conducive to the promotion and application of clean energies.</p>
         
         <p>In recent decades, lithium-ion batteries (LIBs) with high energy density have become a mature energy storage technology.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202411280-bib-0002" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202411280-bib-0002_R_d9736424e508" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2 - Mater. Today">2</a>-<a href="#adfm202411280-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202411280-bib-0005_R_d9736424e508" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Adv. Sustain. Syst.">5</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, growing concerns about the cost and storage capacity of LIBs have prompted the development of other current-carrying ion batteries. Compared to LIBs, sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) have the advantages of high raw material abundance, low cost, and environmental friendliness.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202411280-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202411280-bib-0006_R_d9736424e513" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - Angew. Chem.">6</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> At the same time, sodium ions do not alloy with aluminum, so the lower-priced aluminum foil can be used as a collector in both the positive and negative electrodes, thus further reducing the cost. On the other hand, among the metal elements of the main group I, sodium has the closest physical and chemical properties to lithium, so the research and development process of LIBs and their key materials is a good reference for the development of SIBs technology.</p>
         
         <p>Batteries are primarily composed of three principal components: the cathode, the electrolyte, and the anode. The specific characteristics of the cathode material have a significant impact on the energy density, power density, and cost of the battery. The development of cathode materials determines the application of SIBs.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202411280-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202411280-bib-0007_R_d9736424e521" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Chem. Soc. Rev.">7</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The reported cathode materials are mainly classified into four categories: layered oxides,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202411280-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202411280-bib-0008_R_d9736424e526" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Adv. Energy Mater.">8</a>, <a href="#adfm202411280-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202411280-bib-0009_R_d9736424e526" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - J. Mater. Chem. A">9</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> polyanionic compounds,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202411280-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202411280-bib-0010_R_d9736424e531" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - Angew. Chem.">10</a>-<a href="#adfm202411280-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202411280-bib-0012_R_d9736424e531" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - Nat. Commun.">12</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Prussian blue analogs,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202411280-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202411280-bib-0013_R_d9736424e537" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - J. Power Sources">13</a>, <a href="#adfm202411280-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202411280-bib-0014_R_d9736424e537" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">14</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> and organic-based cathode materials.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202411280-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202411280-bib-0015_R_d9736424e542" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - J. Mater. Chem. A">15</a>-<a href="#adfm202411280-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202411280-bib-0017_R_d9736424e542" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - J. Energy Chem.">17</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Among them, polyanionic cathode materials have been widely studied due to their advantages such as stable structure, higher working voltage, and no risk of gas production. And iron-based polyanionic compounds have become one of the research hotspots in recent years due to their cheap raw materials, good cycling stability, and high operating voltage,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202411280-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202411280-bib-0018_R_d9736424e548" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">18</a>-<a href="#adfm202411280-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202411280-bib-0020_R_d9736424e548" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - J. Alloys Compd.">20</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> with Na<sub>2</sub>Fe<sub>2</sub>(SO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> and Na<sub>4</sub>Fe<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>(P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub>) (NFPP) as typical representatives. Both can also be easily prepared mimicking commercialized LiFePO<sub>4</sub>. Like other polyanionic compounds, these two materials also have the problem of low electronic conductivity. Meanwhile, due to the different preparation methods and modification strategies, the air stability, actual specific capacity, multiplicity performance, and cycling stability of the materials are quite different.</p>
         
         <p>In fact, Na<sub>2</sub>Fe<sub>2</sub>(SO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> and NFPP have been demonstrated as promising in proof-of-concept laboratory experiments, and pilot-scale production is ongoing. But there's no definitive conclusion as to which of the two may be better. In this paper, we will analyze the crystal structure, preparation method, and modification of Na<sub>2</sub>Fe<sub>2</sub>(SO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> and NFPP, discuss the corresponding mechanism and electrochemical properties, compare the advantages and disadvantages of these two cathode materials, discuss which material is more suitable in different working environments, summarize the main challenges, and propose the future research direction and outlook for their practical applications.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adfm202411280-sec-0020">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adfm202411280-sec-0020-title">2 Alluaudite Na<sub>2</sub>Fe<sub>2</sub>(SO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub></h2>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202411280-sec-0030">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adfm202411280-sec-0030-title">2.1 Crystal Structure and Hygroscopic Properties of Na<sub>2</sub>Fe<sub>2</sub>(SO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub></h3>
            
            <p>The structure and electrochemical properties of Na<sub>2</sub>Fe<sub>2</sub>(SO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> were reported in 2014.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202411280-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202411280-bib-0021_R_d9736424e633" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - Nat. Commun.">21</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> It belongs to the monoclinic crystal system, <i>P</i>2<sub>1</sub>/<i>c</i> space group, and has the alluaudite-type AA`BM<sub>2</sub>(XO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> crystal configuration, where A = Na2, A` = Na3, B = Na1, M = Fe, and X = S. <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202411280-fig-0001">1a,b</a> shows the crystal structure of Na<sub>2</sub>Fe<sub>2</sub>(SO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>, where Fe1 and Fe2 are located at the centers of the octahedra (green) formed by six oxygen and both are connected by co-prisms to form Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>10</sub> dimer units. These Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>10</sub> dimers are connected to SO<sub>4</sub> (yellow tetrahedra) by sharing an O vertex to form a 3D framework with Na sites in five to six-coordination to oxygen. The activation energies of sodium ions migration at three different sites were obtained by density functional theory (DFT) calculations. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202411280-fig-0001">1c</a> shows, from left to right, the migration energies of the Na2 site along the <i>c</i>-axis, between the Na2 and Na1 sites, between the Na1 and Na3 sites, and along the <i>c</i>-axis of the Na3 site, respectively. As can be seen from the figure, the migration energy of Na3 along the <i>c</i>-axis is only 0.28&nbsp;eV, with the lowest migration barrier, indicating that Na<sup>+</sup> migration occurs preferentially at the Na3 position in this structure and that the kinetics of ion migration is excellent (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202411280-fig-0001">1d</a>). Due to the extremely strong induced effect of SO<sub>4</sub><sup>2−</sup>, the material has a high operating voltage (≈3.8&nbsp;V vs Na<sup>+</sup>/Na, Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202411280-fig-0001">1e</a>), which is the first among all reported iron-based cathode materials. The obtained capacity is 85% of the theoretical value (120 mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>). The theoretical energy density of 456&nbsp;Wh&nbsp;kg<sup>−1</sup> is higher than those of LiMn<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub> (430&nbsp;Wh&nbsp;kg<sup>−1</sup>) and is slightly below LiFePO<sub>4</sub> (500&nbsp;Wh&nbsp;kg<sup>−1</sup>), suggesting its feasibility for practical application.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adfm202411280-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/7708d88a-d986-459e-95f8-18235a33b91d/adfm202411280-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/7708d88a-d986-459e-95f8-18235a33b91d/adfm202411280-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/d895d682-2e54-4904-89de-76ca4ca6123a/adfm202411280-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/7708d88a-d986-459e-95f8-18235a33b91d/adfm202411280-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202411280-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202411280" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">First study of Na<sub>2</sub>Fe<sub>2</sub>(SO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>. a,b) Crystal structure diagram, c) Na<sup>+</sup> migration activation energy, d) Difference of the bond valence sum from the ideal value, e) Galvanostatic charge-discharge (GCD) profiles at a rate of C/20 at 25 °C. Inset: The dQ/dV. Reproduced with permission.<sup>[</sup><span class="figureLink bibLink tab-link"><sup><a href="#adfm202411280-bib-0021" class="figureLink bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202411280-bib-0021_R_d9736424e747" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - Nat. Commun.">21</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Copyright 2014, Nature Publishing Group.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>With in-depth research, the structure of this alluaudite shows strong compositional tunability, allowing for the formation of a series of sodium-rich phases, sodium-poor phases, or the replacement of iron sites by other transition metals and keeping the basic lattice structure unchanged.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202411280-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202411280-bib-0022_R_d9736424e755" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - ChemElectroChem">22</a>-<a href="#adfm202411280-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202411280-bib-0024_R_d9736424e755" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - J. Mater. Chem. A">24</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> It was also found that, like other sulphate-based materials,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202411280-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202411280-bib-0025_R_d9736424e760" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - J. Phys. Chem. C">25</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> this material has the disadvantage of being prone to water absorption due to the Lewis basicity of oxygen atom in SO<sub>4</sub><sup>2−</sup> commensurate with that of H<sub>2</sub>O. Barman et&nbsp;al.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202411280-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202411280-bib-0026_R_d9736424e771" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - J. Solid State Electrochem.">26</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> exposed the synthesized alluaudite phase Na<sub>2.27</sub>Fe<sub>1.86</sub>(SO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> to different relative humidity, and the structure was determined by X-ray different (XRD) (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202411280-fig-0002">2a</a>). It was found that with the increase of relative humidity, the sodium-rich phase Na<sub>2.27</sub>Fe<sub>1.86</sub>(SO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> will gradually phase change into the bloedite phase Na<sub>2</sub>Fe(SO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>·4H<sub>2</sub>O. Thermochemistry measurements revealed the enthalpies of formation (ΔH°<sub>f</sub>) of alluaudite and bloedite are exothermic. Hydrated bloedite (ΔH°<sub>f</sub>&nbsp;=&nbsp;−117.16&nbsp;±&nbsp;1.10&nbsp;kJ&nbsp;mol<sup>−1</sup>) was found to be significantly more energetically stable than anhydrous alluaudite (ΔH°<sub>f</sub>&nbsp;=&nbsp;−11.76&nbsp;±&nbsp;1.25&nbsp;kJ&nbsp;mol<sup>−1</sup>). Thus alluaudite sulfate was found to be prone to moisture attack leading to the formation of hydrated derivatives. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202411280-fig-0002">2b</a>, the bloedite phase has no channels for reversible migration of sodium ions and the periphery is encapsulated by water molecules. The increase in the potential energy of the sodium ions to exit from the cathode and enter into the solution will lead to an increase in the internal resistance of the cell, which will affect the overall electrochemical performance. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202411280-fig-0002">2c</a> depicts that reversible transformation of the alluaudite phase with the bloedite phase can be achieved by simple annealing and hydration. Therefore, how to find a more suitable synthesis method for the synthesis of Na<sub>2</sub>Fe<sub>2</sub>(SO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> by avoiding contact with water in the air as much as possible has become one of the research highlights.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adfm202411280-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/8d6d46a3-0d4f-4b0b-bc66-3be31578655f/adfm202411280-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/8d6d46a3-0d4f-4b0b-bc66-3be31578655f/adfm202411280-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/f8407bc5-dff0-4240-944a-bad2da964143/adfm202411280-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/8d6d46a3-0d4f-4b0b-bc66-3be31578655f/adfm202411280-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">

                     </div>

                  </figcaption>

            </section>
         </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202411280-sec-0040">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adfm202411280-sec-0040-title">2.2 Preparation of Na<sub>2</sub>Fe<sub>2</sub>(SO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub></h3>
            
            <p>Similar to LiFePO<sub>4</sub> cathode in LIBs, the preparation methods of Na<sub>2</sub>Fe<sub>2</sub>(SO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> are mainly divided into two main categories, that is, solid-state reaction and liquid-phase method (spray-drying, freeze-drying, ion-thermal method, etc.). <b>Table</b> <a class="tableLink scrollableLink" title="Link to table" href="#adfm202411280-tbl-0001">1</a><sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202411280-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202411280-bib-0027_R_d9736424e890" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - Adv. Energy Mater.">27</a>-<a href="#adfm202411280-bib-0034" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202411280-bib-0034_R_d9736424e890" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 34 - Small">34</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> lists the studies on Na<sub>2</sub>Fe<sub>2</sub>(SO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> cathode materials in recent years. The liquid-phase method usually does not require dehydration of the FeSO<sub>4</sub>·7H<sub>2</sub>O feedstock or does not require complete dehydration, thus saving time and energy consumption.</p>

               </div>
               
               
               
               <div class="article-section__table-footnotes">
                  <ul>
                     <li id="adfm202411280-tbl1-note-0001">1C ≈ 120&nbsp;mA&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>.</li>
                  </ul>
               </div>
               <div class="article-section__table-source"></div>
            </div>
            
            <p>A representative study was reported by Plewa<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202411280-bib-0035" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202411280-bib-0035_R_d9736424e1531" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 35 - J. Mater. Chem. A">35</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> et&nbsp;al., in which stoichiometric Na<sub>2</sub>FeM(SO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>/C (M = Fe, Mn, Ni) was synthesized with a discharge capacity of 110.0 mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> (theoretical capacity of 120 mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>) by the liquid-phase method, which still maintains a capacity retention of &gt;90% after 50 cycles. Meanwhile, a sodium-ion full cell was constructed based on Na<sub>2</sub>FeM(SO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>/C cathode, TiO<sub>2</sub> anode, and 1&nbsp;<span class="smallCaps">m</span> NaPF<sub>6</sub> (EC: DEC = 1:1) electrolyte. The TiO<sub>2</sub>|Na<sup>+</sup>|Na<sub>2</sub>FeM(SO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>/C full cell operated at a voltage of ≈3.0&nbsp;V, with a discharge capacity of 66 mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> (about half of that of the half-cell), having an energy density of 200&nbsp;Wh&nbsp;kg<sup>−1</sup> without the need for sodium embedding in the negative electrode material, which indicates that the Na<sub>2</sub>FeM(SO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>/C material has good application prospects.</p>
            
            <p>Dwibedi<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202411280-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202411280-bib-0028_R_d9736424e1584" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">28</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> et&nbsp;al. synthesized a homogeneous nanostructured sodium-rich phase Na<sub>2.4</sub>Fe<sub>1.8</sub>(SO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> product by a low-temperature ionothermal synthesis at 300&nbsp;°C, with a first-cycle specific capacity of 80 mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.05C.</p>
            
            <p>The solid-state reaction is simpler to operate compared to the liquid-phase method. Plewa<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202411280-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202411280-bib-0031_R_d9736424e1604" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31 - Solid State Ion.">31</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> used a low-temperature solid-state method, in which pretreated anhydrous FeSO<sub>4</sub> and anhydrous Na<sub>2</sub>SO<sub>4</sub> were ball-milled for 30&nbsp;min at 100&nbsp;°C three times, and then sintered under the protection of a reducing atmosphere at 350&nbsp;°C for 24&nbsp;h. The Na<sub>2.5</sub>Fe<sub>1.75</sub>(SO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> product, with a particle size distribution mainly ≈100&nbsp;nm, was obtained. The specific capacity of the first discharge at 0.1C was 80 mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>.</p>
            
            <p>It was found that the Na<sub>2</sub>Fe<sub>2</sub>(SO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> phase in stoichiometric ratio is always difficult to synthesize or the purity of the product is not high enough. Most syntheses generate the sodium-rich phase Na<sub>2+2x</sub>Fe<sub>2-x</sub>(SO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> with a non-stoichiometric ratio (x ≈ 0.25). Thomas et&nbsp;al.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202411280-bib-0036" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202411280-bib-0036_R_d9736424e1646" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 36 - J. Mater. Chem. A">36</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> found that the Fe<sup>2+</sup> in the Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>10</sub> dimer produces strong repulsion due to its proximity and that the alluaudite Na<sub>2</sub>Fe<sub>2</sub>(SO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> phase is mesostable, with Na<sup>+</sup> occupying not only A, A`, and B sites but also a small amount of the ortho-octahedral M site, resulting in the sodium-iron atomic ratio always being greater than one.</p>
            
            <p>Dwibedi et&nbsp;al.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202411280-bib-0037" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202411280-bib-0037_R_d9736424e1672" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 37 - MRS Adv">37</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> used a liquid phase synthesis method to prepare the intermediate phases of sodium sulfate and ferrous sulfate by first using citric acid and vitamin C as a chelating agent and antioxidant, respectively, and then the intermediate phases were kept warm at 200&nbsp;°C to prepare the samples with sodium-iron ratios varying from 1 to 2. It was found that the sodium-rich phases with non-stoichiometric ratios were synthesized mainly by this low-temperature method, and the products had good chemical stability. However, the decrease in Fe<sup>2+</sup> content led to a decrease in the theoretical specific capacity from 120 to 106 mAh&nbsp;g (x = 0.25).</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202411280-sec-0050">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adfm202411280-sec-0050-title">2.3 Modification of Na<sub>2</sub>Fe<sub>2</sub>(SO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub></h3>
            
            <p>The pure phase Na<sub>2</sub>Fe<sub>2</sub>(SO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> material has poor electrical conductivity and insufficient air stability. To improve the utility of this material, some modification means are needed to enhance its performance. Drawing on LIBs cathode materials, conventional modification methods are mainly of the following types: one type is structural modulation of unit cells, which is mainly achieved by partial elemental substitution in the structure of iron-based polyanion-type compounds. Elemental doping can adjust the composition of the electrode material at the atomic level, change the material's band gap,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202411280-bib-0038" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202411280-bib-0038_R_d9736424e1703" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 38 - Adv. Energy Mater.">38</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> and improve the intrinsic conductivity of the electrode. In iron-based sulfate compounds, elemental doping such as Ni and Mn is usually used for structural tuning. Plewa doped Na<sub>2</sub>FeM(SO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> with nickel and manganese elements, and the discharge capacity is shown in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202411280-fig-0003">3a</a>, which shows that the discharge capacity of both nickel and manganese doped cathode materials is slightly higher than that of undoped samples. Meanwhile, the fundamental merits of Na<sub>2</sub>FeM(SO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> vary with the composition, such as lattice parameters and electronic conductivity (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202411280-fig-0003">3b,c</a>). Araujo et&nbsp;al.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202411280-bib-0039" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202411280-bib-0039_R_d9736424e1731" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 39 - Phys. Chem. Chem. Phys.">39</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> further studied the family by investigating Na<sub>2</sub>M<sub>2</sub>(SO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> (M = Fe, Ni, Mn). According to the DFT study, the introduction of Ni and Mn ions into the lattice of Na<sub>2</sub>M<sub>2</sub>(SO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> leads to the <i>p</i>-orbitals of the oxygen being closer to the Fermi level (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202411280-fig-0003">3d–f</a>), and it is hypothesized that oxygen atoms close to the Fermi energy effectively participate in the electrode reaction through anionic redox, leading to a small elevation of capacity.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adfm202411280-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/0c9b70d0-68d1-4b47-9b83-89db92a6e9fb/adfm202411280-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/0c9b70d0-68d1-4b47-9b83-89db92a6e9fb/adfm202411280-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/0e5ea3e7-4bdc-4f13-98ee-7de382ee963e/adfm202411280-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/0c9b70d0-68d1-4b47-9b83-89db92a6e9fb/adfm202411280-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202411280-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202411280" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a–c) Comparison of Na<sub>2</sub>FeM(SO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>/C (M = Fe, Ni, Mn) and Na<sub>2</sub>Fe<sub>2</sub>(SO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> in terms of a) Discharge curves of the first three cycles at 0.1C, b) lattice parameter, and c) electrical conductivity. a–c) Reproduced with permission.<sup>[</sup><span class="figureLink bibLink tab-link"><sup><a href="#adfm202411280-bib-0035" class="figureLink bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202411280-bib-0035_R_d9736424e1794" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 35 - J. Mater. Chem. A">35</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Copyright 2020, Materials Chemistry A. State of the density of d) Na<sub>2</sub>Fe<sub>2</sub>(SO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>, e) Na<sub>2</sub>Mn<sub>2</sub>(SO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>, and f) Na<sub>2</sub>Ni<sub>2</sub>(SO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>. d–f) Reproduced with permission.<sup>[</sup><span class="figureLink bibLink tab-link"><sup><a href="#adfm202411280-bib-0039" class="figureLink bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202411280-bib-0039_R_d9736424e1825" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 39 - Phys. Chem. Chem. Phys.">39</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Copyright 2016, Royal Society of Chemistry.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>Another modification means is carbon coating. By introducing one or more layers of carbon outside the material, can effectively enhance the electronic conductivity on the surface of the active particles, and also inhibit the agglomeration of the particles, reduce the size of the particles, and shorten the ionic transport distance,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202411280-bib-0040" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202411280-bib-0040_R_d9736424e1833" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 40 - New J. Chem.">40</a>-<a href="#adfm202411280-bib-0042" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202411280-bib-0042_R_d9736424e1833" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 42 - Small">42</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> to enhance the multiplicity performance of the electrode material. Chen et&nbsp;al.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202411280-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202411280-bib-0027_R_d9736424e1838" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - Adv. Energy Mater.">27</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> synthesized graphene oxide-coated Na<sub>2</sub>Fe<sub>2</sub>(SO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>@C@GO by freeze-drying method, which can provide a discharge capacity of 107.9 mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.1C, and still has &gt;90% capacity retention after 300 cycles at 0.2C. Wang<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202411280-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202411280-bib-0029_R_d9736424e1854" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - J. Mater. Chem. A">29</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> et&nbsp;al. synthesized nitrogen-doped graphene coated on the surface of iron-based sulfate to obtain the Na<sub>2</sub>Fe<sub>2</sub>(SO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>@N-GO cathode material by solvent-thermal method and succeeded in increasing the sodium ion diffusion coefficient (D<sub>Na+</sub>) from 1.03&nbsp;×&nbsp;10<sup>−14</sup> to 1.24 × 10<sup>−13</sup> cm<sup>2</sup>&nbsp;s<sup>−1</sup>, and the charge transfer resistance (R<sub>ct</sub>) was reduced from 579.7 to 120.7 Ω. Hou et&nbsp;al.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202411280-bib-0043" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202411280-bib-0043_R_d9736424e1881" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 43 - J. Power Sources">43</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> synthesised carbon-coated Na<sub>2.4</sub>Fe<sub>1.8</sub>(SO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>/C by the solid-state method. The nano complexes are in a diameter of ≈100&nbsp;nm, and the conductivity of the material was successfully increased to 7.91 × 10<sup>−2</sup> S&nbsp;cm<sup>−2</sup> with a discharge capacity of 100.2 mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at 1C and &gt;95% capacity retention after 100 cycles. The introduction of heteroatoms, such as N, B, and S mono- or polyatomic co-doping, into the structure of the coating carbon can enhance the electrical conductivity of the carbon layer while introducing defects to further improve the electron conduction and ion diffusion of the material.</p>
            
            <p>Meso-structural modulation is also an important means to improve the electrochemical properties of materials. Recently, Zhang et&nbsp;al.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202411280-bib-0044" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202411280-bib-0044_R_d9736424e1905" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 44 - Nat. Commun.">44</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> improved the sodium ion storage properties by modulating the heterostructure and exposed crystalline surfaces of the material. As shown in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202411280-fig-0004">4a,b</a>, the existence of grain boundaries inside the particles can be seen by high-angle dark-field scanning transmission electron microscopy (HAADF-STEM), and the diffusion process of sodium ions on both sides of the grain boundaries can be divided into two kinds: diffusion in the Na<sub>2.26</sub>Fe<sub>1.87</sub>(SO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> phase represented by the blue region and diffusion in the Na<sub>6</sub>Fe(SO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>4</sub> phase represented by the red region, the former with a higher operating voltage and the latter with faster ion diffusion. By physicochemical characterization and theoretical calculations, it is shown that the addition of the heterogeneous structure of the Na<sub>6</sub>Fe(SO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>4</sub> phase to Na<sub>2.26</sub>Fe<sub>1.87</sub>(SO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> densifies the sodium ion migration channels and lowers the energy barriers (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202411280-fig-0004">4c</a>) to facilitate the ion dynamics. In laboratory-scale pouch cell tests in a pre-sodiumated FeS/carbon anode combination (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202411280-fig-0004">4d–f</a>), the Na<sub>2.26</sub>Fe<sub>1.87</sub>(SO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>-based cathode had an initial discharge capacity of ≈83.9 mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, an average discharge voltage of 2.35&nbsp;V, and a specific capacity retention of ≈97% after 40 cycles at 24&nbsp;mA&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adfm202411280-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/afe2d3d3-d822-4154-89df-1697193eecde/adfm202411280-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/afe2d3d3-d822-4154-89df-1697193eecde/adfm202411280-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/403e1cdd-13af-4512-ba4e-cb063d1db660/adfm202411280-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/afe2d3d3-d822-4154-89df-1697193eecde/adfm202411280-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">

                  </figcaption>

            </section>
         </section>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adfm202411280-sec-0060">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adfm202411280-sec-0060-title">3 NASICON-type Na<sub>4</sub>Fe<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>(P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub>)</h2>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202411280-sec-0070">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adfm202411280-sec-0070-title">3.1 Crystal structure of Na<sub>4</sub>Fe<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>(P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub>)</h3>
            
            <p>The crystal structure of Na<sub>4</sub>Me<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>(P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub>) (Me = Mn, Co, Ni, Mg) has been reported for a long time,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202411280-bib-0045" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202411280-bib-0045_R_d9736424e2049" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 45 - J. Power Sources">45</a>-<a href="#adfm202411280-bib-0048" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202411280-bib-0048_R_d9736424e2049" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 48 - J. Alloys Compd.">48</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> and NFPP cathode material with isomorphic heterogeneity was reported for the first time by Kim et&nbsp;al.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202411280-bib-0049" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202411280-bib-0049_R_d9736424e2054" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 49 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">49</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> in 2012. In term of thermodynamic stability, NFPP is more stable than its cobalt and nickel analogs.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202411280-bib-0050" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202411280-bib-0050_R_d9736424e2060" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 50 - J. Phys. Chem. C">50</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> As shown in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202411280-fig-0005">5a</a>, the crystal structure of NFPP consists of a 3D network of infinite layers of [Fe<sub>3</sub>P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>13</sub>]<sub>∞</sub> parallel to the bc plane, and the [Fe<sub>3</sub>P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>13</sub>]<sub>∞</sub> layers are connected along the a-axis by P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub> groups. This pyrophosphate connection creates a large number of channels where Na ions can diffuse reversibly along the b axis. It was determined to belong to the <i>Pn</i>2<sub>1</sub><i>a</i> space group by Rietveld refinement with cell parameters a = 18.07517 Å, b = 6.53238 Å, c = 10.64760 Å, and V = 1257.204 Å<sup>3</sup>. There are four Na sites in the crystal structure and they are interconnected by a 3D framework. Two Na sites (Na2 and Na3), forming into NaO<sub>7</sub> polyhedra and NaO<sub>6</sub> octahedra, are present along the a axis. The rest two Na sites (Na1 and Na4), in the form of NaO<sub>6</sub> octahedra, are located in the bc plane. Fe sites form FeO<sub>6</sub> polyhedra, two of the three FeO<sub>6</sub> polyhedra forms Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>10</sub> dimers while the rest is connected to the dimer by sharing one common O. Kim et&nbsp;al. calculated all possible diffusion paths among these four Na sites by first-principles calculations, and the gradation of colors from yellow to green in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202411280-fig-0005">5b</a> indicates the high and low energies that are required for Na to migrate along the paths, from which it is possible to determine the Na<sup>+</sup> rapid diffusion pathway. Although all Na sites are connected by rather low activation energies, Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion in the large channel along the b-axis (Na1-Na1) possesses the lowest activation energy. At the same time, the diffusion of Na<sup>+</sup> along the b axis shows a sinusoidal motion due to the electrostatic repulsion of the neighboring Fe elements.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adfm202411280-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/42b1fb22-4904-4895-ba9c-968554758755/adfm202411280-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/42b1fb22-4904-4895-ba9c-968554758755/adfm202411280-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/eecb171b-18f1-4813-8bbe-1abe6c7a59cf/adfm202411280-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/42b1fb22-4904-4895-ba9c-968554758755/adfm202411280-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 5<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202411280-fig-0005&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202411280" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">The first study of NFPP. a) Unit cell. b) 3D sodium ion diffusion path in NFPP structure. c) GCD profile under a C/40 rate and the calculated average voltage at each region. The inset shows the dQ/dV curve of initial charge/discharge profiles. Integrated spin as a function of integration radius around Fe in Na<sub>4–</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>Fe<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>(P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub>) d) <i>x</i> = 0, and e) <i>x</i> = 3. Reproduced with permission.<sup>[</sup><span class="figureLink bibLink tab-link"><sup><a href="#adfm202411280-bib-0049" class="figureLink bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202411280-bib-0049_R_d9736424e2164" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 49 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">49</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Copyright 2012, American Chemical Society.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>Similar to Na<sub>2</sub>Fe<sub>2</sub>(SO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>, NFPP, as a polyanionic sodium storage cathode material, has displayed excellent cycling stability, but the induced effect of phosphate on Fe<sup>2+</sup> is not as strong as that of sulfate, so the discharge voltage of NFPP cathode material is lower than that of Na<sub>2</sub>Fe<sub>2</sub>(SO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> (NFPP: 3.2&nbsp;V vs Na/Na<sup>+</sup>, Na<sub>2</sub>Fe<sub>2</sub>(SO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> 3.8&nbsp;V vs Na/Na<sup>+</sup>) (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202411280-fig-0005">5c</a>). The magnetic studies illustrated that the Fe valence state changed along with the Na migration (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202411280-fig-0005">5d,e</a>). In addition, NFPP may be more suitable for high-temperature application environments because phosphate groups are more thermally stable than sulfate groups (SO<sub>4</sub><sup>2−</sup> decomposition occurs at ≈400&nbsp;°C to produce SO<sub>2</sub>). The decomposition temperature for NFPP is ≈530&nbsp;°C, yielding different products depending on the remaining Na content in the phases.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202411280-bib-0051" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202411280-bib-0051_R_d9736424e2216" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 51 - Chem. Mater.">51</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
            
            <p>The ion migration kinetics are in-depth studied by DFT calculations.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202411280-bib-0052" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202411280-bib-0052_R_d9736424e2223" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 52 - Nat. Commun.">52</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The result illustrated that the most favorable diffusion tunnel in the first stage is along the a direction with an extremely small energy barrier (0.02&nbsp;eV) (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202411280-fig-0006">6a,b</a>), while the rest paths may all allow ion migration, linking into 3D diffusion pathways (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202411280-fig-0006">6a–d</a>). The work also investigated temperature-dependent electrochemistry. At −20&nbsp;°C, the specific capacity of NFPP could reach 95.0 mAhg<sup>−1</sup> and 84.7 mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.1&nbsp;C and 0.2&nbsp;C, respectively, while the capacity has little difference between 50&nbsp;°C and room temperature (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202411280-fig-0006">6e</a>). The cycling stability at −20 and 50&nbsp;°C was impressive, with 92.1% and 91.4% capacity retention at 0.5&nbsp;C, respectively. The analyses of lattice evolution based on in situ synchrotron-based XRD patterns revealed a volume change of only 4.0% (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202411280-fig-0006">6f,g</a>), thus guaranteeing long-term cycling stability up to 4400 cycles. In addition, the structure of NFPP was not significantly altered after exposure to air for three months, indicating its good air stability.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adfm202411280-fig-0006"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/66a48dea-fec0-482e-9707-83a44eed6004/adfm202411280-fig-0006-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/66a48dea-fec0-482e-9707-83a44eed6004/adfm202411280-fig-0006-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/bdf32658-323b-4e8e-abd7-ca45d419317e/adfm202411280-fig-0006-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/66a48dea-fec0-482e-9707-83a44eed6004/adfm202411280-fig-0006-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 6<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202411280-fig-0006&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202411280" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Kinetic investigation of NFPP. a) Image of the same Na<sup>+</sup> ion groups, and b) the corresponding migration energy barriers. c) Image of the different Na<sup>+</sup> ion groups, and d) the corresponding migration energy barriers. e) Rate performance at different temperatures. f,g) Lattice parameter evolution during the charge/discharge process. Commun.">52</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Copyright 2019, Nature Publishing Group.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
         </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202411280-sec-0080">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adfm202411280-sec-0080-title">3.2 Preparation of Na<sub>4</sub>Fe<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>(P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub>)</h3>
            
            <p>Differences in synthesis methods as well as the type and content of the synthesized material can lead to differences in the sodium content of the material, with sodium-rich Na<sub>2</sub>Fe<sub>2</sub>(SO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> phase being more stable and therefore it is more likely to produce compounds with a non-stoichiometric ratio (Na:Fe&gt;1:1), whereas NFPP is more stable in stoichiometry and the products are usually stoichiometric NFPP phase. The main methods for the preparation of NFPP are the liquid-phase method (spray-drying), solid-phase method, sol-gel method, and electrospinning technique. The liquid-phase method is to dissolve sodium salt (e.g., NaH<sub>2</sub>PO<sub>4</sub>·2H<sub>2</sub>O) and iron salt (e.g., Fe(NO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>3</sub>·9H<sub>2</sub>O) with specific stoichiometric ratio in deionized water, stirring to make them fully mixed at the ionic level, and then subsequently dispersed into particles by mechanical mixing. In contact with hot air, the water evaporates rapidly over a large area, and most of the water is removed instantly through the cyclone. The dry precursor is obtained through the separation of cyclone separator. Subsequently, the precursor is ball-milled and then sintered and annealed at a high temperature (mostly ≈550&nbsp;°C) under protective gas to obtain the final product.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202411280-bib-0053" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202411280-bib-0053_R_d9736424e2321" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 53 - Carbon">53</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In contrast, the solid-phase method usually involves ball milling of dehydrated sodium and iron salts, drying, and then high-temperature sintering and annealing under vacuum or protective gas (hydrogen-argon mixture) to obtain the products.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202411280-bib-0054" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202411280-bib-0054_R_d9736424e2326" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 54 - J. Mater. Chem. A">54</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
            
            <p>In the liquid-phase method study, Zhao et al.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202411280-bib-0055" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202411280-bib-0055_R_d9736424e2333" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 55 - ACS Energy Lett.">55</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> studied a total of 17 samples in the prepared Na<sub>4</sub>Fe<sub>x</sub>P<sub>4</sub>O<sub>12+x</sub>(2≤x≤4) system by spray-drying method, and the phases and structural evolutions were revealed by both computational and experimental methods. As shown in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202411280-fig-0007">7a,b</a>, in the Na<sub>4</sub>Fe<sub>x</sub>P<sub>4</sub>O<sub>12+x</sub>(2≤x≤4) system, three main phases exist, Na<sub>2</sub>FeP<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub> (NFPO), NFPP, and NaFePO<sub>4</sub> (NFP), with NFPP having the highest formation energy, and the relative content of each substance changes as x varies from 2.0 to 4.0 during the synthesis process. Among NFPPs with different x, when x = 2.91 pure phase Na<sub>4</sub>Fe<sub>2.91</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>(P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub>) containing Fe defects can be obtained. When x is slightly larger or smaller than 2.91, NFP and/or NFPO impurities are easily formed due to the lower formation energy of the Fe2 site, which is more likely to be lost in the structure. As a result, the x = 2.91 sample exhibited the best electrochemical performance in terms of the initial capacity despite of the low initial Coulombic efficiency, as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202411280-fig-0007">7c–e</a>. The cycling stability of Na<sub>4</sub>Fe<sub>x</sub>P<sub>4</sub>O<sub>12+x</sub> (2 ≤ x ≤ 4) with different x values is excellent, with almost no capacity decay after 1000 cycles at 1&nbsp;C. Notably, Na<sub>4</sub>Fe<sub>2.91</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>(P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub>) displayed the highest specific capacity of 110 mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adfm202411280-fig-0007"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/405f0d6a-7dde-4a19-87f0-584f3e619ce7/adfm202411280-fig-0007-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/405f0d6a-7dde-4a19-87f0-584f3e619ce7/adfm202411280-fig-0007-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/41a20c66-8169-4dde-ba11-ecebb27c9812/adfm202411280-fig-0007-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/405f0d6a-7dde-4a19-87f0-584f3e619ce7/adfm202411280-fig-0007-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 7<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202411280-fig-0007&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202411280" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Investigation of Na<sub>4</sub>Fe<sub>x</sub>P<sub>4</sub>O<sub>12+x</sub> (2 ≤ x ≤ 4). a) The crystal structures of NFPO, NFPP, and NFP. b) The phase diagram of structure evolution with x from 2.0 to 4.0. c) CV curves from 2 to 4&nbsp;V at a scan rate of 2&nbsp;mV&nbsp;s<sup>−1</sup>. d) The first cycle charge/discharge curves. e) The summarized charge/discharge capacities. Reproduced with permission.<sup>[</sup><span class="figureLink bibLink tab-link"><sup><a href="#adfm202411280-bib-0055" class="figureLink bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202411280-bib-0055_R_d9736424e2441" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 55 - ACS Energy Lett.">55</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Copyright 2023, American Chemical Society.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>Electrospinning technology involves transferring a precursor solution into a plastic syringe, setting a certain solution feed rate, controlled by a syringe pump, and raising the cathode voltage (aluminum foil) and anode voltage (needle) to initiate electrospinning. Meanwhile, the humidity of the spinning process was strictly controlled below 40%. Aluminum foil grounding was used to collect the nanofibres, and then the collected films were first stabilized in the air at a constant temperature for several hours, followed by high-temperature carbonization in a hydrogen-argon mixed atmosphere to obtain NFPP/C nanofibers.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202411280-bib-0056" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202411280-bib-0056_R_d9736424e2449" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 56 - Energy Storage Mater.">56</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The electrospinning technique eliminates the step of ball milling compared to solid- and liquid-phase methods and the cellular structure of the product can be well preserved. Zhang<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202411280-bib-0057" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202411280-bib-0057_R_d9736424e2454" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 57 - J. Power Sources">57</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> et&nbsp;al. synthesized NFPP nanoparticles embedded in carbon nanoribbons by using poly(vinylpyrrolidone)-assisted electrospinning, and obtained a capacity as high as 128.6 mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.1&nbsp;C (1&nbsp;C = 128.9 mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>), high rate performance (61.2 mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at 50 C), and ultra-long cycle life (72% capacity retention after 5000 cycles at 50 C). Meanwhile, the NFPP nanoribbons also exhibited excellent low-temperature performance, providing a discharge capacity of 84.5 mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at 15&nbsp;°C and 0.05&nbsp;C.</p>
            
            <p>The sol–gel method starts with a solution containing pure precursors, transforms the solution into a sol by hydrolysis and condensation reactions, and thermally treats the gel at relatively low temperatures to obtain a solid material.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202411280-bib-0058" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202411280-bib-0058_R_d9736424e2471" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 58 - J. Sol-Gel Sci. Technol.">58</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Usually, the sol–gel method employs only phosphate as the phosphorus source in the synthesis of Na<sub>4</sub>Fe<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub>, and the thermal conversion process of PO<sub>4</sub>→P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub> can form the NFPP framework.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202411280-bib-0059" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202411280-bib-0059_R_d9736424e2496" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 59 - ACS Appl. Energy Mater.">59</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Wu et&nbsp;al.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202411280-bib-0060" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202411280-bib-0060_R_d9736424e2501" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 60 - J. Power Sources">60</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> synthesized hybrid polyanionic NaFPP/C nanocomposites by the sol–gel method. The raw phosphate material was converted into mixed phosphate-pyrophosphate with high phase purity through a self-condensation reaction at 500&nbsp;°C. Most of the peaks in the in situ XRD spectra during the charging and discharging process exhibited solid-solution reactions (<b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202411280-fig-0008">8a</a>), but obvious local lattice distortions could be observed at the end of the charging process. The solid-state NMR data (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202411280-fig-0008">8b</a>) showed that a localized sodium extraction took place following the structural transformation, that is, the charging and discharging process is an incomplete solid solution reaction. NFPP/C was able to provide an initial capacity of 110 mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> with an average discharge voltage close to 3.1&nbsp;V at 0.05&nbsp;C. At a rate of 10&nbsp;C, the material provided a discharge capacity of 78 mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at 25&nbsp;°C and 82 mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at 55&nbsp;°C (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202411280-fig-0008">8c</a>).</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adfm202411280-fig-0008"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/eaff59c4-83b1-4061-97ce-f0833976e28c/adfm202411280-fig-0008-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/eaff59c4-83b1-4061-97ce-f0833976e28c/adfm202411280-fig-0008-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/a10d5bad-de06-402e-96d5-6bab14ec2b94/adfm202411280-fig-0008-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/eaff59c4-83b1-4061-97ce-f0833976e28c/adfm202411280-fig-0008-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 8<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202411280-fig-0008&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202411280" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) In situ XRD patterns and corresponding charge–discharge curves of the NFPP/C electrode during the first cycle. b) <sup>23</sup>Na MAS NMR spectra of the NFPP/C electrode at different charged states. c) The comparison of rate performance in different sintering temperature. a–c) Reproduced with permission.<sup>[</sup><span class="figureLink bibLink tab-link"><sup><a href="#adfm202411280-bib-0060" class="figureLink bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202411280-bib-0060_R_d9736424e2548" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 60 - J. Power Sources">60</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Copyright 2016, Power Sources. d) Cyclic stability of NFPP/ prepared at different pH, at 50&nbsp;mA&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, and e,f) the corresponding charge–discharge curves at high rates. d–f) Reproduced with permission.<sup>[</sup><span class="figureLink bibLink tab-link"><sup><a href="#adfm202411280-bib-0061" class="figureLink bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202411280-bib-0061_R_d9736424e2555" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 61 - Electrochim. Acta">61</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Copyright 2024, Elsevier B.V.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>Aleksandra<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202411280-bib-0061" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202411280-bib-0061_R_d9736424e2564" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 61 - Electrochim. Acta">61</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> prepared a series of samples with different NFPP/NFPO ratios by varying the ratio of citric acid to iron using Na<sub>4</sub>P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub> and NH<sub>4</sub>H<sub>2</sub>PO<sub>4</sub> as reactants for the first time and found that the formation of the polyanionic phase was affected by the amount of the initial citric acid as well as by the pH of the suspension. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202411280-fig-0008">8d–f</a>, the specific capacity of NFPP is 42 mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at pH = 3. When the pH of the reaction suspension is adjusted to a neutral value, the NFPP phase with the largest specific capacity can be obtained, which has a higher sodium storage performance than that of the NFPP/NFP heterostructure and can reach the theoretical capacity (129 mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>) at a relatively high current (1 A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>).</p>
            
            <p>In addition, Chen<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202411280-bib-0062" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202411280-bib-0062_R_d9736424e2595" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 62 - EcoMat">62</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> et&nbsp;al. adopted a low-cost synthesis method of “killing two birds with one stone”, in which they used the rust on the surface of carbon steel as the iron source, and dissolved the rust with an acidic mixed solution (NaH<sub>2</sub>PO<sub>4</sub>-citric acid), which not only serves as the carbon source of the cathode material but also passivates the surface of the carbon steel that prevents rust. Then the obtained suspension was sand milled, spray dried, and sintered, and cooled to obtain porous hollow spherical NFPP cathode materials with an average particle size of 0.103&nbsp;µm. The ion diffusion coefficient of the NFPP obtained by this method in the de-sodiation process was 3.6&nbsp;×&nbsp;10<sup>−11</sup> cm<sup>2</sup>&nbsp;s<sup>−1</sup>. The full cell combined with hard carbon possessed an energy density of 102.5&nbsp;Wh&nbsp;kg<sup>−1</sup> and a capacity retention of 92.5% after 1000 cycles.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202411280-sec-0090">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adfm202411280-sec-0090-title">3.3 Modification of Na<sub>4</sub>Fe<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>(P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub>)</h3>
            
            <p>NFPP has shown poor electrical conductivity and large volume changes in charge/discharge and therefore needs further modification to improve its electrochemical performance. <b>Table</b> <a class="tableLink scrollableLink" title="Link to table" href="#adfm202411280-tbl-0002">2</a><sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202411280-bib-0063" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202411280-bib-0063_R_d9736424e2639" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 63 - Nano Energy">63</a>-<a href="#adfm202411280-bib-0070" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202411280-bib-0070_R_d9736424e2639" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 70 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">70</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> lists the research on NFPP in recent years, from which it can be seen that the cathode materials synthesized by various methods have a very high specific capacity (theoretical specific capacity of 129 mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>), and also have rate performance. In the study of Gao et&nbsp;al.,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202411280-bib-0066" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202411280-bib-0066_R_d9736424e2647" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 66 - Chem. Eng. J.">66</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> the capacity of the prepared NFPP cathode material is almost undecayed during cycling for 200 cycles at 1&nbsp;C under 60&nbsp;°C, which proves that this material has very excellent cycling stability.</p>

                        <tr>
